      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Music">
        Music
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Jamaica" title="Music of Jamaica">
        Music of Jamaica
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Though a small nation, Jamaican culture has a strong global presence. The musical genres
       <a href="/wiki/Reggae" title="Reggae">
        reggae
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ska" title="Ska">
        ska
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Mento" title="Mento">
        mento
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Rocksteady" title="Rocksteady">
        rocksteady
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Dub_music" title="Dub music">
        dub
       </a>
       , and, more recently,
       <a href="/wiki/Dancehall" title="Dancehall">
        dancehall
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Ragga" title="Ragga">
        ragga
       </a>
       all originated in the island's vibrant, popular urban recording industry. Jamaica also played an important role in the development of
       <a href="/wiki/Punk_rock" title="Punk rock">
        punk rock
       </a>
       , through reggae and ska. Reggae has also influenced American
       <a href="/wiki/Hip_hop_music" title="Hip hop music">
        rap
       </a>
       music, as they share roots as rhythmic, African styles of music. Some rappers, such as
       <a href="/wiki/The_Notorious_B.I.G." title="The Notorious B.I.G.">
        The Notorious B.I.G.
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Busta_Rhymes" title="Busta Rhymes">
        Busta Rhymes
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Heavy_D" title="Heavy D">
        Heavy D
       </a>
       , are of Jamaican descent. Internationally known reggae musician
       <a href="/wiki/Bob_Marley" title="Bob Marley">
        Bob Marley
       </a>
       was also Jamaican.
      </p>
      <p>
       Many other internationally known artists were born in Jamaica, including
       <a href="/wiki/Millie_Small" title="Millie Small">
        Millie Small
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Lee_%22Scratch%22_Perry" title='Lee "Scratch" Perry'>
        Lee "Scratch" Perry
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Gregory_Isaacs" title="Gregory Isaacs">
        Gregory Isaacs
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Half_Pint" title="Half Pint">
        Half Pint
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Protoje" title="Protoje">
        Protoje
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Peter_Tosh" title="Peter Tosh">
        Peter Tosh
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bunny_Wailer" title="Bunny Wailer">
        Bunny Wailer
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Big_Youth" title="Big Youth">
        Big Youth
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Cliff" title="Jimmy Cliff">
        Jimmy Cliff
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Brown" title="Dennis Brown">
        Dennis Brown
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Desmond_Dekker" title="Desmond Dekker">
        Desmond Dekker
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Beres_Hammond" title="Beres Hammond">
        Beres Hammond
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Beenie_Man" title="Beenie Man">
        Beenie Man
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Shaggy_(musician)" title="Shaggy (musician)">
        Shaggy
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Grace_Jones" title="Grace Jones">
        Grace Jones
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Shabba_Ranks" title="Shabba Ranks">
        Shabba Ranks
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Super_Cat" title="Super Cat">
        Super Cat
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Buju_Banton" title="Buju Banton">
        Buju Banton
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sean_Paul" title="Sean Paul">
        Sean Paul
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/I_Wayne" title="I Wayne">
        I Wayne
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bounty_Killer" title="Bounty Killer">
        Bounty Killer
       </a>
       and many others. Bands that came from Jamaica include
       <a href="/wiki/Black_Uhuru" title="Black Uhuru">
        Black Uhuru
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third_World_Band" title="Third World Band">
        Third World Band
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inner_Circle_(reggae_band)" title="Inner Circle (reggae band)">
        Inner Circle
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chalice_Reggae_Band" title="Chalice Reggae Band">
        Chalice Reggae Band
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Culture_(band)" title="Culture (band)">
        Culture
       </a>
       , Fab Five and
       <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Heritage" title="Morgan Heritage">
        Morgan Heritage
       </a>
       . The genre
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oldschool_jungle" title="Oldschool jungle">
        jungle
       </a>
       emerged from London's Jamaican diaspora. The birth of
       <a href="/wiki/Hip_hop_music" title="Hip hop music">
        hip-hop
       </a>
       in New York City owed much to the city's Jamaican community.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">
        Literature
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Jamaican_literature" title="Jamaican literature">
        Jamaican literature
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Ian_Fleming" title="Ian Fleming">
        Ian Fleming
       </a>
       , who lived in Jamaica, repeatedly used the island as a setting in his
       <a href="/wiki/James_Bond" title="James Bond">
        James Bond
       </a>
       novels, including
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Live_and_Let_Die_(novel)" title="Live and Let Die (novel)">
         Live and Let Die
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Dr._No_(novel)" title="Dr. No (novel)">
         Doctor No
        </a>
       </i>
       , "
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/For_Your_Eyes_Only_(short_story)" title="For Your Eyes Only (short story)">
        For Your Eyes Only
       </a>
       ",
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Man_with_the_Golden_Gun_(novel)" title="The Man with the Golden Gun (novel)">
         The Man with the Golden Gun
        </a>
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Octopussy_and_The_Living_Daylights" title="Octopussy and The Living Daylights">
         Octopussy and The Living Daylights
        </a>
       </i>
       . In addition, James Bond uses a Jamaica-based cover in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Casino_Royale_(novel)" title="Casino Royale (novel)">
         Casino Royale
        </a>
       </i>
       . So far, the only James Bond film adaptation to have been set in Jamaica is
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Dr._No_(film)" title="Dr. No (film)">
         Doctor No
        </a>
       </i>
       . Filming for the fictional island of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_Bond_locations#Locations_depicted_in_films" title="James Bond locations">
        San Monique
       </a>
       in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Live_and_Let_Die_(film)" title="Live and Let Die (film)">
         Live and Let Die
        </a>
       </i>
       took place in Jamaica.
      </p>
      <p>
       The journalist and author
       <a href="/wiki/H._G._de_Lisser" title="H. G. de Lisser">
        H. G. de Lisser
       </a>
       (1878–1944) used his native country as the setting for his many novels. Born in
       <a href="/wiki/Falmouth,_Jamaica" title="Falmouth, Jamaica">
        Falmouth, Jamaica
       </a>
       , de Lisser worked as a reporter for the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Jamaica_Times" title="Jamaica Times">
         Jamaica Times
        </a>
       </i>
       at a young age and in 1920 began publishing the magazine
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planters%27_Punch" title="Planters' Punch">
         Planters' Punch
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <i>
        The White Witch of Rosehall
       </i>
       is one of his better-known novels. He was named Honorary President of the Jamaican Press Association; he worked throughout his professional career to promote the Jamaican sugar industry.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Marlon_James_(novelist)" title="Marlon James (novelist)">
        Marlon James
       </a>
       (1970), novelist has published three novels:
       <i>
        John Crow's Devil
       </i>
       (2005),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Book_of_Night_Women" title="The Book of Night Women">
         The Book of Night Women
        </a>
       </i>
       (2009) and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/A_Brief_History_of_Seven_Killings" title="A Brief History of Seven Killings">
         A Brief History of Seven Killings
        </a>
       </i>
       (2014), winner of the 2015
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Man_Booker_Prize" title="Man Booker Prize">
        Man Booker Prize
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Film">
        Film
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Jamaican_films" title="List of Jamaican films">
        List of Jamaican films
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The cinema actor
       <a href="/wiki/Errol_Flynn" title="Errol Flynn">
        Errol Flynn
       </a>
       lived with his third wife
       <a href="/wiki/Patrice_Wymore" title="Patrice Wymore">
        Patrice Wymore
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Port_Antonio" title="Port Antonio">
        Port Antonio
       </a>
       in the 1950s. He helped develop tourism to this area, popularising trips down rivers on bamboo rafts.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
        <a href="#cite_note-90">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Jamaica has a long history in the film industry dating from the early 1960s. A look at delinquent youth in Jamaica is presented in the 1970s musical crime film
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Harder_They_Come" title="The Harder They Come">
         The Harder They Come
        </a>
       </i>
       , starring
       <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Cliff" title="Jimmy Cliff">
        Jimmy Cliff
       </a>
       as a frustrated (and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychopathic" title="Psychopathic">
        psychopathic
       </a>
       ) reggae musician who descends into a murderous crime spree. The American film
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Cocktail_(1988_film)" title="Cocktail (1988 film)">
         Cocktail
        </a>
       </i>
       (1988), starring
       <a href="/wiki/Tom_Cruise" title="Tom Cruise">
        Tom Cruise
       </a>
       , is one of the more popular films to depict Jamaica. Another popular Jamaican-based film is the 1993 Disney comedy
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Cool_Runnings" title="Cool Runnings">
         Cool Runnings
        </a>
       </i>
       , which is loosely based on the true story of Jamaica's first bobsled team trying to make it in the Winter Olympics.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">
        Cuisine
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Jamaican_cuisine" title="Jamaican cuisine">
        Jamaican cuisine
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The island is famous for its
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jamaican_jerk_spice" title="Jamaican jerk spice">
        Jamaican jerk spice
       </a>
       , which is integral to Jamaican cuisine. Jamaica is also home to
       <a href="/wiki/Red_Stripe" title="Red Stripe">
        Red Stripe
       </a>
       beer and
       <a href="/wiki/Jamaican_Blue_Mountain_Coffee" title="Jamaican Blue Mountain Coffee">
        Jamaican Blue Mountain Coffee
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="National_symbols">
        National symbols
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       (
       <i>
        From the Jamaica Information Service
       </i>
       )
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
        <a href="#cite_note-91">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        National bird:
        <a href="/wiki/Red-billed_streamertail" title="Red-billed streamertail">
         red-billed streamertail
        </a>
        (also called doctor bird) (a
        <a href="/wiki/Hummingbird" title="Hummingbird">
         hummingbird
        </a>
        ,
        <i>
         Trochilus polytmus
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        National flower –
        <a href="/wiki/Lignum_vitae" title="Lignum vitae">
         lignum vitae
        </a>
        (
        <i>
         Guiacum officinale
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        National tree:
        <a href="/wiki/Talipariti_elatum" title="Talipariti elatum">
         blue mahoe
        </a>
        (
        <i>
         Hibiscus talipariti elatum
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        National fruit:
        <a href="/wiki/Ackee" title="Ackee">
         ackee
        </a>
        (
        <i>
         Blighia sapida
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        National motto: "Out of Many, One People."
       </li>
      </ul>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jamaica_motto.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="1920" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Jamaica_motto.jpg/220px-Jamaica_motto.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Jamaica_motto.jpg/330px-Jamaica_motto.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Jamaica_motto.jpg/440px-Jamaica_motto.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jamaica_motto.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Jamaica motto on a building at Papine High School in Kingston, Jamaica.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sport">
        Sport
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_Jamaica" title="Sport in Jamaica">
        Sport in Jamaica
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Usain_Bolt_16082009_Berlin.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="426" data-file-width="640" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Usain_Bolt_16082009_Berlin.JPG/220px-Usain_Bolt_16082009_Berlin.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Usain_Bolt_16082009_Berlin.JPG/330px-Usain_Bolt_16082009_Berlin.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Usain_Bolt_16082009_Berlin.JPG/440px-Usain_Bolt_16082009_Berlin.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Usain_Bolt_16082009_Berlin.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Usain_Bolt" title="Usain Bolt">
          Usain Bolt
         </a>
         at the
         <a href="/wiki/2009_World_Championships_in_Athletics" title="2009 World Championships in Athletics">
          2009 World Championships in Athletics
         </a>
         in
         <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">
          Berlin
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Sport is an integral part of national life in Jamaica and the island's athletes tend to perform to a standard well above what might ordinarily be expected of such a small country.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-track_92-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-track-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       While the most popular local sport is
       <a href="/wiki/Cricket" title="Cricket">
        cricket
       </a>
       , on the international stage Jamaicans have tended to do particularly well at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Track_and_field_athletics" title="Track and field athletics">
        track and field athletics
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-track_92-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-track-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jamsports_93-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-jamsports-93">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Jamaica has produced some of the world's most famous cricketers, including
       <a href="/wiki/George_Headley" title="George Headley">
        George Headley
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Courtney_Walsh" title="Courtney Walsh">
        Courtney Walsh
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Michael_Holding" title="Michael Holding">
        Michael Holding
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
        <a href="#cite_note-94">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The country was one of the venues of
       <a href="/wiki/2007_Cricket_World_Cup" title="2007 Cricket World Cup">
        2007 Cricket World Cup
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/West_Indies_cricket_team" title="West Indies cricket team">
        West Indies cricket team
       </a>
       is one of 10
       <a href="/wiki/International_Cricket_Council" title="International Cricket Council">
        ICC
       </a>
       full member teams that participate in international
       <a href="/wiki/Test_cricket" title="Test cricket">
        Test cricket
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cricket_95-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-cricket-95">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Jamaica_national_cricket_team" title="Jamaica national cricket team">
        Jamaica national cricket team
       </a>
       competes regionally, and also provides players for the West Indies team.
       <a href="/wiki/Sabina_Park" title="Sabina Park">
        Sabina Park
       </a>
       is the only Test venue in the island, but the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenfield_Stadium_(Trelawny)" title="Greenfield Stadium (Trelawny)">
        Greenfield Stadium
       </a>
       is also used for cricket.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cricketgrounds_96-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-cricketgrounds-96">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenfield_97-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Greenfield-97">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Chris_Gayle" title="Chris Gayle">
        Chris Gayle
       </a>
       is the most renowned batsman from Jamaica currently representing the West Indies cricket team.
      </p>
      <p>
       Since independence Jamaica has consistently produced world class athletes in track and field.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-track_92-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-track-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In Jamaica involvement in athletics begins at a very young age and most high schools maintain rigorous athletics programs with their top athletes competing in national competitions (most notably the VMBS Girls and Boys Athletics Championships) and international meets (most notably the
       <a href="/wiki/Penn_Relays" title="Penn Relays">
        Penn Relays
       </a>
       ). In Jamaica it is not uncommon for young athletes to attain press coverage and national fame long before they arrive on the international athletics stage.
      </p>
      <p>
       Over the past six decades Jamaica has produced dozens of world class sprinters including Olympic and World Champion
       <a href="/wiki/Usain_Bolt" title="Usain Bolt">
        Usain Bolt
       </a>
       , world record holder in the 100m for men at 9.58s, and 200m for men at 19.19s. Other noteworthy Jamaican sprinters include
       <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Wint" title="Arthur Wint">
        Arthur Wint
       </a>
       , the first Jamaican Olympic gold medalist;
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Donald_Quarrie" title="Donald Quarrie">
        Donald Quarrie
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Elaine_Thompson" title="Elaine Thompson">
        Elaine Thompson
       </a>
       double Olympic champion from Rio 2016 in the 100m and 200m, Olympic Champion and former 200m world record holder;
       <a href="/wiki/Roy_Anthony_Bridge" title="Roy Anthony Bridge">
        Roy Anthony Bridge
       </a>
       , part of the International Olympic Committee;
       <a href="/wiki/Merlene_Ottey" title="Merlene Ottey">
        Merlene Ottey
       </a>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Delloreen_Ennis-London" title="Delloreen Ennis-London">
        Delloreen Ennis-London
       </a>
       ;
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shelly-Ann_Fraser" title="Shelly-Ann Fraser">
        Shelly-Ann Fraser-Pryce
       </a>
       , the former World and two time Olympic 100m Champion;
       <a href="/wiki/Kerron_Stewart" title="Kerron Stewart">
        Kerron Stewart
       </a>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Aleen_Bailey" title="Aleen Bailey">
        Aleen Bailey
       </a>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Juliet_Cuthbert" title="Juliet Cuthbert">
        Juliet Cuthbert
       </a>
       ; three-time Olympic gold medalist;
       <a href="/wiki/Veronica_Campbell-Brown" title="Veronica Campbell-Brown">
        Veronica Campbell-Brown
       </a>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Sherone_Simpson" title="Sherone Simpson">
        Sherone Simpson
       </a>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Brigitte_Foster-Hylton" title="Brigitte Foster-Hylton">
        Brigitte Foster-Hylton
       </a>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Yohan_Blake" title="Yohan Blake">
        Yohan Blake
       </a>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Herb_McKenley" title="Herb McKenley">
        Herb McKenley
       </a>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/George_Rhoden" title="George Rhoden">
        George Rhoden
       </a>
       , Olympic gold medalist;
       <a href="/wiki/Deon_Hemmings" title="Deon Hemmings">
        Deon Hemmings
       </a>
       , Olympic gold medalist; as well as
       <a href="/wiki/Asafa_Powell" title="Asafa Powell">
        Asafa Powell
       </a>
       , former 100m world record holder and 2x 100m Olympic finalist and gold medal winner in the men's 2008 Olympic 4 × 100 m.
      </p>
      <p>
       Jamaica has also produced several world class amateur and professional boxers including
       <a href="/wiki/Trevor_Berbick" title="Trevor Berbick">
        Trevor Berbick
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Mike_McCallum" title="Mike McCallum">
        Mike McCallum
       </a>
       . First-generation Jamaican athletes have continued to make a significant impact on the sport internationally, especially in the United Kingdom where the list of top British boxers born in Jamaica or of Jamaican parents includes
       <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_Honeyghan" title="Lloyd Honeyghan">
        Lloyd Honeyghan
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Chris_Eubank" title="Chris Eubank">
        Chris Eubank
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Audley_Harrison" title="Audley Harrison">
        Audley Harrison
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/David_Haye" title="David Haye">
        David Haye
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Lennox_Lewis" title="Lennox Lewis">
        Lennox Lewis
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Frank_Bruno" title="Frank Bruno">
        Frank Bruno
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">
        Association football
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horse-racing" title="Horse-racing">
        horse-racing
       </a>
       are other popular sports in Jamaica. The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jamaica_National_Football_Team" title="Jamaica National Football Team">
        national football team
       </a>
       qualified for the 1998
       <a href="/wiki/FIFA_World_Cup" title="FIFA World Cup">
        FIFA World Cup
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jamaica_national_bobsled_team" title="Jamaica national bobsled team">
        Jamaica national bobsled team
       </a>
       was once a serious contender in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Winter_Olympics" title="Winter Olympics">
        Winter Olympics
       </a>
       , beating many well-established teams. Chess and basketball are widely played in Jamaica and are supported by the Jamaica Chess Federation (JCF) and the Jamaica Basketball Federation (JBF), respectively.
       <a href="/wiki/Netball" title="Netball">
        Netball
       </a>
       is also very popular on the island, with the
       <a href="/wiki/Jamaica_national_netball_team" title="Jamaica national netball team">
        Jamaica national netball team
       </a>
       called The Sunshine Girls consistently ranking in the top five in the world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
        <a href="#cite_note-98">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Jamaica_national_rugby_league_team" title="Jamaica national rugby league team">
        Jamaica national rugby league team
       </a>
       is made up of players who play in Jamaica, and UK-players from professional and semi professional teams in the UK.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
        <a href="#cite_note-99">
         [99]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Their first international was a 37–22 loss to the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_national_rugby_league_team" title="United States national rugby league team">
        United States national rugby league team
       </a>
       in November 2009.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
        <a href="#cite_note-100">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Rugby league in Jamaica is growing with universities and high schools taking up the sport.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
        <a href="#cite_note-101">
         [101]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
        <a href="#cite_note-102">
         [102]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The JRLA Championship is the main rugby league competition in the country.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
        <a href="#cite_note-103">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Hurricanes_Rugby_League" title="Hurricanes Rugby League">
        Hurricanes Rugby League
       </a>
       are a professional rugby league team who are hoping to compete in either the
       <a href="/wiki/USA_Rugby_League" title="USA Rugby League">
        USA Rugby League
       </a>
       or the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AMNRL" title="AMNRL">
        AMNRL
       </a>
       by 2013 during that time they will be training young players aged 14–19 who will be part of the Hurricanes RL Academy in the hope of developing into full-time professional players.
      </p>
      <p>
       According to
       <a href="/wiki/ESPN" title="ESPN">
        ESPN
       </a>
       , the highest paid Jamaican professional athlete in 2011 was
       <a href="/wiki/Justin_Masterson" title="Justin Masterson">
        Justin Masterson
       </a>
       , starting pitcher for the Cleveland Indians.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
        <a href="#cite_note-104">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Education">
        Education
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Jamaica" title="Education in Jamaica">
        Education in Jamaica
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The emancipation of the slaves heralded in the establishment of the Jamaican education system for the masses. Prior to emancipation there were few schools for educating locals. Many sent their children off to England to access quality education.
      </p>
      <p>
       After emancipation the West Indian Commission granted a sum of money to establish Elementary Schools, now known as
       <i>
        All Age Schools
       </i>
       . Most of these schools were established by the churches.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
        <a href="#cite_note-105">
         [105]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This was the genesis of the modern Jamaican school system.
      </p>
      <p>
       Presently the following categories of schools exist:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <b>
         Early childhood
        </b>
        – Basic, infant and privately operated pre-school. Age cohort: 2 – 5 years.
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Primary
        </b>
        – Publicly and privately owned (privately owned being called preparatory schools). Ages 3 – 12 years.
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Secondary
        </b>
        – Publicly and privately owned. Ages 10 – 19 years. The high schools in Jamaica may be either single-sex or co-educational institutions, and many schools follow the traditional
        <a href="/wiki/Sixth_form" title="Sixth form">
         English grammar school model
        </a>
        used throughout the British West Indies.
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Tertiary
        </b>
        – Community colleges; teachers' colleges, with the Mico Teachers' College (now The MICO University College) being the oldest, founded in 1836; the Shortwood Teachers' College (which was once an all-female teacher training institution); vocational training centres, colleges and universities, publicly and privately owned. There are five local universities: the
        <a href="/wiki/University_of_the_West_Indies" title="University of the West Indies">
         University of the West Indies
        </a>
        (Mona Campus); the
        <a href="/wiki/University_of_Technology,_Jamaica" title="University of Technology, Jamaica">
         University of Technology, Jamaica
        </a>
        , formerly The College of Art Science and Technology (CAST); the
        <a href="/wiki/Northern_Caribbean_University" title="Northern Caribbean University">
         Northern Caribbean University
        </a>
        , formerly West Indies College; the
        <a href="/wiki/University_College_of_The_Caribbean" title="University College of The Caribbean">
         University College of The Caribbean
        </a>
        ; and the International University of the Caribbean.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Additionally, there are many community and teacher training colleges.
      </p>
      <p>
       Education is free from the early childhood to secondary levels. There are also opportunities for those who cannot afford further education in the vocational arena, through the Human Employment and Resource Training-National Training Agency (HEART Trust-NTA) programme,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
        <a href="#cite_note-106">
         [106]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which is opened to all working age national population
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
        <a href="#cite_note-107">
         [107]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and through an extensive scholarship network for the various universities.
      </p>
      <p>
       Students are taught Spanish in school from the primary level upwards; about 40–45% of educated people in Jamaica knows some form of Spanish.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">
        Economy
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Jamaica" title="Economy of Jamaica">
        Economy of Jamaica
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Negril-Beach.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Negril-Beach.jpg/220px-Negril-Beach.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Negril-Beach.jpg/330px-Negril-Beach.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Negril-Beach.jpg/440px-Negril-Beach.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Negril-Beach.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A beach in
         <a href="/wiki/Negril" title="Negril">
          Negril
         </a>
         with a hotel and restaurant
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JamesBondBeach2.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/JamesBondBeach2.jpg/220px-JamesBondBeach2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/JamesBondBeach2.jpg/330px-JamesBondBeach2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/JamesBondBeach2.jpg/440px-JamesBondBeach2.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:JamesBondBeach2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         James Bond Beach in
         <a href="/wiki/Oracabessa" title="Oracabessa">
          Oracabessa
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Jamaica is a
       <a href="/wiki/Mixed_economy" title="Mixed economy">
        mixed economy
       </a>
       with both state enterprises and private sector businesses. Major sectors of the Jamaican economy include
       <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">
        agriculture
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">
        mining
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing" title="Manufacturing">
        manufacturing
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tourism" title="Tourism">
        tourism
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Financial" title="Financial">
        financial
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Insurance" title="Insurance">
        insurance
       </a>
       services. Tourism and mining are the leading earners of
       <a href="/wiki/Foreign_exchange_market" title="Foreign exchange market">
        foreign exchange
       </a>
       . Half the Jamaican economy relies on services, with half of its income coming from services such as tourism. An estimated 1.3 million foreign tourists visit Jamaica every year.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
        <a href="#cite_note-108">
         [108]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Supported by multilateral financial institutions, Jamaica has, since the early 1980s, sought to implement structural reforms aimed at fostering private sector activity and increasing the role of market forces in resource allocation. Since 1991, the government has followed a programme of economic liberalisation and stabilisation by removing exchange controls, floating the exchange rate, cutting
       <a href="/wiki/Tariff" title="Tariff">
        tariffs
       </a>
       , stabilising the Jamaican currency, reducing
       <a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">
        inflation
       </a>
       and removing restrictions on
       <a href="/wiki/Foreign_direct_investment" title="Foreign direct investment">
        foreign investment
       </a>
       . Emphasis has been placed on maintaining strict fiscal discipline, greater openness to trade and financial flows, market liberalisation and reduction in the size of government. During this period, a large share of the economy was returned to private sector ownership through
       <a href="/wiki/Divestment" title="Divestment">
        divestment
       </a>
       and privatisation programmes.
      </p>
      <p>
       The macroeconomic stabilisation programme introduced in 1991, which focused on tight fiscal and monetary policies, has contributed to a controlled reduction in the rate of inflation. The annual inflation rate decreased from a high of 80.2% in 1991 to 7.9% in 1998. Inflation for FY1998/99 was 6.2% compared to 7.2% in the corresponding period in CUU1997/98. The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Government_of_Jamaica" title="Government of Jamaica">
        Government of Jamaica
       </a>
       remains committed to lowering inflation, with a long-term objective of bringing it in line with that of its major trading partners.
      </p>
      <p>
       After a period of steady growth from 1985 to 1995, real
       <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">
        GDP
       </a>
       decreased by 1.8% and 2.4% in 1996 and 1997, respectively. The decrease in GDP in 1996 and 1997 was largely due to significant problems in the financial sector and, in 1997, a severe island-wide drought (the worst in 70 years) that drastically reduced agricultural production. In 1997, nominal GDP was approximately J$220,556.2 million (US$6,198.9 million based on the average annual exchange rate of the period).
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alligator_pond_Jamaica_fishing_boats_gm.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Alligator_pond_Jamaica_fishing_boats_gm.jpg/220px-Alligator_pond_Jamaica_fishing_boats_gm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Alligator_pond_Jamaica_fishing_boats_gm.jpg/330px-Alligator_pond_Jamaica_fishing_boats_gm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Alligator_pond_Jamaica_fishing_boats_gm.jpg/440px-Alligator_pond_Jamaica_fishing_boats_gm.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alligator_pond_Jamaica_fishing_boats_gm.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Fishing boats and
         <a href="/wiki/Bauxite" title="Bauxite">
          bauxite
         </a>
         <a href="/wiki/Cargo_ship" title="Cargo ship">
          cargo ships
         </a>
         share the waterways near
         <a href="/wiki/Alligator_Pond" title="Alligator Pond">
          Alligator Pond
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The economy in 1997 was marked by low levels of
       <a href="/wiki/Import" title="Import">
        import
       </a>
       growth, high levels of private capital inflows and relative stability in the
       <a href="/wiki/Foreign_exchange_market" title="Foreign exchange market">
        foreign exchange market
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Recent economic performance shows the Jamaican economy is recovering.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agricultural" title="Agricultural">
        Agricultural
       </a>
       production, an important engine of growth increased 15.3% in third quarter of 1998 compared to the corresponding period in 1997, signaling the first positive growth rate in the sector since January 1997.
       <a href="/wiki/Bauxite" title="Bauxite">
        Bauxite
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alumina" title="Alumina">
        alumina
       </a>
       production increased 5.5% from January to December, 1998 compared to the corresponding period in 1997. January's bauxite production recorded a 7.1% increase relative to January 1998 and continued expansion of alumina production through 2009 is planned by
       <a href="/wiki/Alcoa" title="Alcoa">
        Alcoa
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
        <a href="#cite_note-109">
         [109]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Jamaica is the fifth largest exporter of bauxite in the world, after Australia, China, Brazil and Guinea. Tourism, which is the largest foreign exchange earner, showed improvement as well. In the third quarter of 1998, growth in tourist arrivals accelerated with an overall increase of 8.5% in tourism earnings in 1998 when compared to the corresponding period in 1997. Jamaica's agricultural exports are
       <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">
        sugar
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bananas" title="Bananas">
        bananas
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Coffee" title="Coffee">
        coffee
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Rum" title="Rum">
        rum
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Yam_(vegetable)" title="Yam (vegetable)">
        yams
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Jamaica has a wide variety of industrial and commercial activities. The
       <a href="/wiki/Aviation" title="Aviation">
        aviation
       </a>
       industry is able to perform most routine aircraft maintenance, except for heavy structural repairs. There is a considerable amount of technical support for transport and agricultural aviation. Jamaica has a considerable amount of
       <a href="/wiki/Industrial_engineering" title="Industrial engineering">
        industrial engineering
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_manufacturing" title="Light manufacturing">
        light manufacturing
       </a>
       , including
       <a href="/wiki/Metal_fabrication" title="Metal fabrication">
        metal fabrication
       </a>
       , metal roofing, and furniture
       <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing" title="Manufacturing">
        manufacturing
       </a>
       . Food and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beverage" title="Beverage">
        beverage
       </a>
       processing,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glassware" title="Glassware">
        glassware
       </a>
       manufacturing,
       <a href="/wiki/Software" title="Software">
        software
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Data_processing" title="Data processing">
        data processing
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Printing" title="Printing">
        printing
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Publishing" title="Publishing">
        publishing
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Insurance" title="Insurance">
        insurance
       </a>
       underwriting,
       <a href="/wiki/Music" title="Music">
        music
       </a>
       and recording, and advanced
       <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">
        education
       </a>
       activities can be found in the larger urban areas. The Jamaican
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Construction_industry" title="Construction industry">
        construction industry
       </a>
       is entirely self-sufficient, with professional technical standards and guidance.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">
        <a href="#cite_note-110">
         [110]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Since the first quarter of 2006, the economy of Jamaica has undergone a period of staunch growth. With inflation for the 2006 calendar year down to 6.0% and unemployment down to 8.9%, the nominal GDP grew by an unprecedented 2.9%.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
        <a href="#cite_note-111">
         [111]
        </a>
       </sup>
       An investment programme in island transportation and utility infrastructure and gains in the tourism, mining, and service sectors all contributed this figure. All projections for 2007 show an even higher potential for economic growth with all estimates over 3.0% and hampered only by urban crime and public policies.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2006, Jamaica became part of the
       <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Community" title="Caribbean Community">
        CARICOM
       </a>
       Single Market and Economy
       <a href="/wiki/CARICOM_Single_Market_and_Economy" title="CARICOM Single Market and Economy">
        (CSME)
       </a>
       as one of the pioneering members.
      </p>
      <p>
       The global economic downturn had a significant impact on the Jamaican economy for the years 2007 to 2009, resulting in negative economic growth. The government implemented a new Debt Management Initiative, the Jamaica Debt Exchange (JDX) on 14 January 2010. The initiative would see holders of Government of Jamaica (GOJ) bonds returning the high interest earning instruments for bonds with lower yields and longer maturities. The offer was taken up by over 95% of local financial institutions and was deemed a success by the government.
      </p>
      <p>
       Owing to the success of the JDX program, the Bruce Golding-led government was successful in entering into a borrowing arrangement with the IMF on 4 February 2010 for the amount of US$1.27b. The loan agreement is for a period of three years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
        <a href="#cite_note-112">
         [112]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In April 2014, the Governments of Jamaica and China signed the preliminary agreements for the first phase of the Jamaican Logistics Hub (JLH) – the initiative that aims to position Kingston as the fourth node in the global logistics chain, joining Rotterdam, Dubai and Singapore, and serving the Americas.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">
        <a href="#cite_note-113">
         [113]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Project, when completed, is expected to provide many jobs for Jamaicans, Economic Zones for multinational companies
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114">
        <a href="#cite_note-114">
         [114]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and much needed economic growth to alleviate the country's heavy
       <a href="/wiki/Debt-to-GDP_ratio" title="Debt-to-GDP ratio">
        debt-to-GDP ratio
       </a>
       . Strict adherence to the IMF's refinancing programme and preparations for the JLH has favourably affected Jamaica's credit rating and outlook from the three biggest rating agencies.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Infrastructure">
        Infrastructure
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">
        Transport
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Transport_in_Jamaica" title="Transport in Jamaica">
        Transport in Jamaica
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Halfway_Tree_Transport_Center,_Kingston,_Jamaica.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Halfway_Tree_Transport_Center%2C_Kingston%2C_Jamaica.jpg/220px-Halfway_Tree_Transport_Center%2C_Kingston%2C_Jamaica.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Halfway_Tree_Transport_Center%2C_Kingston%2C_Jamaica.jpg/330px-Halfway_Tree_Transport_Center%2C_Kingston%2C_Jamaica.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Halfway_Tree_Transport_Center%2C_Kingston%2C_Jamaica.jpg/440px-Halfway_Tree_Transport_Center%2C_Kingston%2C_Jamaica.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Halfway_Tree_Transport_Center,_Kingston,_Jamaica.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Halfway Tree Transport Center, Kingston
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transportation" title="Transportation">
        transport
       </a>
       infrastructure in Jamaica consists of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roadway" title="Roadway">
        roadways
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Railway" title="Railway">
        railways
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Aviation" title="Aviation">
        air transport
       </a>
       , with roadways forming the backbone of the island's internal transport system.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Roadways">
        Roadways
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Roads_in_Jamaica" title="Roads in Jamaica">
        Roads in Jamaica
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Jamaican road network consists of almost 21,000 kilometres (13,000 mi) of roads, of which over 15,000 kilometres (9,300 mi) is paved.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WorldFactbook_1-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-WorldFactbook-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Government_of_Jamaica" title="Government of Jamaica">
        Jamaican Government
       </a>
       has, since the late 1990s and in cooperation with private investors, embarked on a campaign of infrastructural improvement projects, one of which includes the creation of a system of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freeway" title="Freeway">
        freeways
       </a>
       , the first such access-controlled roadways of their kind on the island, connecting the main population centres of the island. This project has so far seen the completion of 33 kilometres (21 mi) of freeway.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Railways">
        Railways
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Railways_of_Jamaica" title="Railways of Jamaica">
        Railways of Jamaica
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Railways in Jamaica no longer enjoy the prominent position they once did, having been largely replaced by roadways as the primary means of transport. Of the 272 kilometres (169 mi) of railway found in Jamaica, only 57 kilometres (35 mi) remain in operation, currently used to transport
       <a href="/wiki/Bauxite" title="Bauxite">
        bauxite
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WorldFactbook_1-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-WorldFactbook-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On 13 April 2011, limited passenger service was resumed between May Pen, Spanish Town and Linstead.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Air_transport">
        Air transport
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Montego_Bay_plane_Photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1424" data-file-width="2144" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Montego_Bay_plane_Photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg/220px-Montego_Bay_plane_Photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Montego_Bay_plane_Photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg/330px-Montego_Bay_plane_Photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Montego_Bay_plane_Photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg/440px-Montego_Bay_plane_Photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Montego_Bay_plane_Photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A
         <a href="/wiki/US_Airways" title="US Airways">
          US Airways
         </a>
         aircraft landing at Montego Bay (2013)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       There are three international airports in Jamaica with modern
       <a href="/wiki/Airport_terminal" title="Airport terminal">
        terminals
       </a>
       , long
       <a href="/wiki/Runway" title="Runway">
        runways
       </a>
       , and the navigational equipment required to accommodate the large
       <a href="/wiki/Jet_aircraft" title="Jet aircraft">
        jet aircraft
       </a>
       used in modern and
       <a href="/wiki/Air_travel" title="Air travel">
        air travel
       </a>
       :
       <a href="/wiki/Norman_Manley_International_Airport" title="Norman Manley International Airport">
        Norman Manley International Airport
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Kingston,_Jamaica" title="Kingston, Jamaica">
        Kingston
       </a>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Ian_Fleming_International_Airport" title="Ian Fleming International Airport">
        Ian Fleming International Airport
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Boscobel,_Jamaica" title="Boscobel, Jamaica">
        Boscobel
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Saint_Mary_Parish,_Jamaica" title="Saint Mary Parish, Jamaica">
        Saint Mary Parish
       </a>
       ; and the island's largest and busiest airport,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Donald_Sangster_International_Airport" title="Sir Donald Sangster International Airport">
        Sir Donald Sangster International Airport
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Resort" title="Resort">
        resort
       </a>
       city of
       <a href="/wiki/Montego_Bay" title="Montego Bay">
        Montego Bay
       </a>
       . Manley and Sangster International airports are home to the country's national airline,
       <a href="/wiki/Air_Jamaica" title="Air Jamaica">
        Air Jamaica
       </a>
       . In addition there are local commuter airports at
       <a href="/wiki/Tinson_Pen_Aerodrome" title="Tinson Pen Aerodrome">
        Tinson Pen (Kingston)
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Port_Antonio" title="Port Antonio">
        Port Antonio
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Negril" title="Negril">
        Negril
       </a>
       , which cater to internal flights only. Many other small, rural centres are served by private fields on sugar estates or bauxite mines.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span id="Ports.2C_shipping_and_lighthouses">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ports,_shipping_and_lighthouses">
        Ports, shipping and lighthouses
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lighthouses_in_Jamaica" title="Lighthouses in Jamaica">
        Lighthouses in Jamaica
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Owing to its location in the Caribbean Sea in the
       <a href="/wiki/Sea_lane" title="Sea lane">
        shipping lane
       </a>
       to the
       <a href="/wiki/Panama_Canal" title="Panama Canal">
        Panama Canal
       </a>
       and relative proximity to large markets in North America and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emerging_market" title="Emerging market">
        emerging markets
       </a>
       in Latin America, Jamaica receives high
       <a href="/wiki/Containerization" title="Containerization">
        container
       </a>
       traffic. The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Container_terminal" title="Container terminal">
        container terminal
       </a>
       at the Port of Kingston has undergone large expansion in capacity in recent years to handle growth both already realised as well as that which is projected in coming years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nameJamaicaObserver_115-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-nameJamaicaObserver-115">
         [115]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Montego Freeport in
       <a href="/wiki/Montego_Bay" title="Montego Bay">
        Montego Bay
       </a>
       also handles a variety of cargo like (though more limited than) the Port of Kingston, mainly agricultural products.
      </p>
      <p>
       There are several other ports positioned around the island, including Port Esquivel in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Catherine_Parish,_Jamaica" title="Saint Catherine Parish, Jamaica">
        St. Catherine
       </a>
       (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WINDALCO" title="WINDALCO">
        WINDALCO
       </a>
       ), Rocky Point in
       <a href="/wiki/Clarendon_Parish,_Jamaica" title="Clarendon Parish, Jamaica">
        Clarendon
       </a>
       , Port Kaiser in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Elizabeth_Parish,_Jamaica" title="Saint Elizabeth Parish, Jamaica">
        St. Elizabeth
       </a>
       , Port Rhoades in Discovery Bay, Reynolds Pier in
       <a href="/wiki/Ocho_Rios" title="Ocho Rios">
        Ocho Rios
       </a>
       , and Boundbrook Port in
       <a href="/wiki/Port_Antonio" title="Port Antonio">
        Port Antonio
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       To aid the navigation of shipping, Jamaica operates nine lighthouses.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Energy">
        Energy
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Jamaica" title="Solar power in Jamaica">
        Solar power in Jamaica
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Jamaica depends on petroleum imports to satisfy its national energy needs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WorldFactbook_1-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-WorldFactbook-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Many test sites have been explored for oil, but no commercially viable quantities have been found.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pcj.com_116-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-pcj.com-116">
         [116]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The most convenient sources of imported oil and motor fuels (diesel, gasoline, and jet fuel) are from
       <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">
        Mexico
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">
        Venezuela
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Jamaica's electrical power is produced by diesel (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bunker_fuel" title="Bunker fuel">
        bunker oil
       </a>
       ) generators located in Old Harbour. Other smaller power stations (most owned by the Jamaica Public Service Company,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
        <a href="#cite_note-117">
         [117]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the island's electricity provider) support the island's electrical grid including the Hunts Bay Power Station, the Bogue Power Station, the Rockfort Power Station and small hydroelectric plants on the White River, Rio Bueno, Morant River, Black River (Maggotty) and Roaring River.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118">
        <a href="#cite_note-118">
         [118]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A wind farm, owned by the Petroleum Corporation of Jamaica, was established at Wigton, Manchester.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119">
        <a href="#cite_note-119">
         [119]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Jamaica has successfully operated a
       <a href="/wiki/SLOWPOKE_reactor" title="SLOWPOKE reactor">
        SLOWPOKE-2 nuclear reactor
       </a>
       of 20 kW capacity since the early 1980s, but there are no plans to expand nuclear power at present.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120">
        <a href="#cite_note-120">
         [120]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Jamaica imports approximately 80,000 barrels (13,000 m
       <sup>
        3
       </sup>
       ) of oil energy products per day,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pcj.com_116-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-pcj.com-116">
         [116]
        </a>
       </sup>
       including asphalt and lubrication products. Just 20% of imported fuels are used for road transportation, the rest being used by the bauxite industry, electricity generation, and aviation. 30,000 barrels/day of crude imports are processed into various motor fuels and asphalt by the Petrojam Refinery in Kingston.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121">
        <a href="#cite_note-121">
         [121]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Jamaica produces enormous quantities of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrous_ethanol" title="Hydrous ethanol">
        drinking alcohol
       </a>
       (at least 5% water content), most of which appears to be consumed as beverages, and none used as motor fuel. Facilities exist to refine hydrous ethanol feedstock into
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absolute_ethanol" title="Absolute ethanol">
        anhydrous ethanol
       </a>
       (0% water content), but as of 2007, the process appeared to be uneconomic and the production plant was idle.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122">
        <a href="#cite_note-122">
         [122]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Water_supply_and_sanitation">
        Water supply and sanitation
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Water_supply_and_sanitation_in_Jamaica" title="Water supply and sanitation in Jamaica">
        Water supply and sanitation in Jamaica
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Water supply and sanitation is characterised by high levels of access to an
       <a href="/wiki/Improved_water_source" title="Improved water source">
        improved water source
       </a>
       , while access to adequate sanitation stands at only 80%. This situation affects especially the poor, including the urban poor many of which live in the country's over 595 unplanned squatter settlements in unhealthy and unsanitary environments with a high risk of
       <a href="/wiki/Waterborne_diseases" title="Waterborne diseases">
        waterborne disease
       </a>
       . Despite a number of policy papers that were mainly focused on water supply and despite various projects funded by external donors, increases in access have remained limited (1% for water and 5% for sanitation between 1990 and 2004).
      </p>
      <p>
       The responsibility for water and sanitation policies within the government rests with the Ministry of Water and Housing, and the main service provider is the National Water Commission. An autonomous regulatory agency, the Office of Utilities Regulation, approves tariffs and establishes targets for efficiency increases.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Communication">
        Communication
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications_in_Jamaica" title="Telecommunications in Jamaica">
        Telecommunications in Jamaica
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Jamaica has a fully
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digital_telephony" title="Digital telephony">
        digital telephone communication system
       </a>
       with a mobile penetration of over 95%.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EIU_123-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-EIU-123">
         [123]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The country's two mobile operators –
       <a href="/wiki/Flow_(brand)" title="Flow (brand)">
        FLOW Jamaica
       </a>
       (formerly LIME, bMobile and Cable and Wireless Jamaica) and
       <a href="/wiki/Digicel" title="Digicel">
        Digicel Jamaica
       </a>
       have spent millions in network upgrades and expansion. The newest operator, Digicel was granted a licence in 2001 to operate mobile services in the newly liberalised telecom market that had once been the sole domain of the incumbent FLOW (then Cable and Wireless Jamaica) monopoly. Digicel opted for the more widely used
       <a href="/wiki/GSM" title="GSM">
        GSM
       </a>
       wireless system, while a past operator, Oceanic (which became Claro Jamaica and later merged with Digicel Jamaica in 2011) opted for the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CDMA" title="CDMA">
        CDMA
       </a>
       standard. FLOW (formerly "LIME" – pre-
       <a href="/wiki/Columbus_Communications" title="Columbus Communications">
        Columbus Communications
       </a>
       merger) which had begun with
       <a href="/wiki/Digital_AMPS" title="Digital AMPS">
        TDMA
       </a>
       standard, subsequently upgraded to GSM in 2002, decommissioned TDMA in 2006 and only utilised that standard until 2009 when LIME launched its 3G network.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124">
        <a href="#cite_note-124">
         [124]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Both operators currently provide islandwide coverage with HSPA+ (3G) technology. Currently, only
       <a href="/wiki/Digicel" title="Digicel">
        Digicel
       </a>
       offers LTE to its customers
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125">
        <a href="#cite_note-125">
         [125]
        </a>
       </sup>
       whereas FLOW Jamaica has committed to launching LTE in the cities of Kingston and Montego Bay, places where Digicel's LTE network is currently only found in, in short order.
      </p>
      <p>
       A new entrant to the Jamaican communications market,
       <a href="/wiki/Columbus_Communications" title="Columbus Communications">
        Flow Jamaica
       </a>
       , laid a new
       <a href="/wiki/Submarine_communications_cable" title="Submarine communications cable">
        submarine cable
       </a>
       connecting Jamaica to the United States. This new cable increases the total number of submarine cables connecting Jamaica to the rest of the world to four. Cable and Wireless Communications (parent company of LIME) acquired the company in late 2014 and replaced their brand LIME with FLOW.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126">
        <a href="#cite_note-126">
         [126]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Flow_(brand)" title="Flow (brand)">
        FLOW Jamaica
       </a>
       currently has the most broadband and cable subscribers on the island and also has 1 million mobile subscribers,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127">
        <a href="#cite_note-127">
         [127]
        </a>
       </sup>
       second to Digicel (which had, at its peak, over 2 million mobile subscriptions on its network).
      </p>
      <p>
       Digicel entered the broadband market in 2010 by offering WiMAX broadband,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128">
        <a href="#cite_note-128">
         [128]
        </a>
       </sup>
       capable of up to 6 Mbit/s per subscriber. To further their broadband share post-LIME/FLOW merger in 2014, the company introduced a new broadband service called Digicel Play,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129">
        <a href="#cite_note-129">
         [129]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which is Jamaica's second
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FTTx" title="FTTx">
        FTTH
       </a>
       offering (after LIME's deployment in selected communities in 2011
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130">
        <a href="#cite_note-130">
         [130]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ). It is currently only available in the parishes of Kingston, Portmore and St. Andrew. It offers speeds of up to 200 Mbit/s down, 100 Mbit/s up via a pure fibre optic network. Digicel's competitor,
       <a href="/wiki/Flow_(brand)" title="Flow (brand)">
        FLOW Jamaica
       </a>
       , has a network consisting of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ADSL" title="ADSL">
        ADSL
       </a>
       , Coaxial and Fibre to the Home (inherited from LIME) and only offers speeds up to 100 Mbit/s. FLOW has committed to expanding its Fibre offering to more areas in order to combat Digicel's entrance into the market.
      </p>
      <p>
       It was announced that the Office and Utilities Regulations (OUR), Ministry of Science, Technology, Energy and Mining (MSTEM) and the Spectrum Management Authority (SMA) have given approval for another mobile operator licence in January 2016.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131">
        <a href="#cite_note-131">
         [131]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The identity of this entrant was ascertained on May 20, 2016, when the Jamaican Government named the new carrier as Symbiote Investments Limited operating under the name
       <a href="/wiki/Caricel" title="Caricel">
        Caricel
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132">
        <a href="#cite_note-132">
         [132]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The company will focus on 4G LTE data offerings and will first go live in the Kingston Metropolitan Area and will expand to the rest of Jamaica thereafter.
      </p>
